# Chapter 1: ACT Math Blueprint — Test Architecture, Scoring Mechanics, and Strategic Mindset

---

## 1.1 The Role of ACT Math in the Overall ACT Examination

The ACT (American College Testing) is a standardized examination used for college admissions across the United States. It serves as a common benchmark that allows admissions officers to compare applicants from vastly different educational backgrounds, school districts, and curricula on a single, uniform scale. The ACT comprises four mandatory multiple-choice sections—English, Mathematics, Reading, and Science—and one optional Writing (essay) section. The mathematics section is the second section of the exam, administered immediately after the 45-minute English section and before the 10-minute intermission.

Understanding the architecture of the ACT Math section is not merely an academic exercise; it is the foundational prerequisite for all targeted preparation. A student who understands exactly how the test is built, how it is scored, and what it rewards will prepare more efficiently, manage time more effectively, and ultimately score higher than a student who simply "does practice problems" without strategic awareness. This chapter provides that architectural blueprint in exhaustive detail.

The ACT Math section tests mathematical concepts that a student should have encountered through the beginning of 12th-grade coursework—typically through Algebra II and some pre-calculus topics. It does not test calculus. It does not test advanced trigonometry beyond the core ratios and identities. What it does test, with remarkable consistency from administration to administration, is mathematical reasoning, the ability to apply known concepts in unfamiliar contexts, and the capacity to work accurately under significant time pressure.

---

## 1.2 Current Test Format: The Enhanced ACT (Post-2020)

In response to evolving testing needs, the ACT introduced an enhanced format beginning in April 2020. This is the current standard format that most test-takers will encounter. Every detail that follows must be internalized.

### Section Parameters

| Parameter | Specification |
|---|---|
| **Number of Questions** | 45 |
| **Time Allotted** | 50 minutes |
| **Answer Choices per Question** | 4 (A/B/C/D or F/G/H/J) |
| **Scored Questions** | Approximately 41 |
| **Unscored Field Test Questions** | Approximately 4 (intermingled, unidentifiable) |
| **Scoring Scale** | 1–36 (scaled score) |
| **Penalty for Wrong Answers** | None |
| **Calculator Policy** | Permitted for the entire section |
| **Reference Sheet** | None provided |
| **Question Arrangement** | Generally increasing in difficulty |

### The Embedded Field Test

One of the most strategically important facts about the ACT Math section is the presence of approximately four unscored "embedded field test" questions. These are experimental questions that ACT, Inc. is field-testing for future exams. They are intermingled among the scored questions and are completely indistinguishable from them. The student has no way to know which questions count and which do not.

**Strategic Implication:** This means that a student should never skip a question or put in minimal effort on the assumption that it "probably doesn't count." Every question should receive full effort. However, if a student encounters a question that seems extraordinarily difficult or covers material that seems outside the normal ACT scope, it *might* be a field test question. This should not change the student's approach—answer every question—but it can provide a small psychological comfort if a particular question feels aberrant.

### The Desmos Calculator Integration

For students taking the digital version of the ACT (available at many test centers), the Desmos graphing calculator is built directly into the testing interface. Desmos is a powerful, free, web-based graphing calculator that can graph functions, solve equations numerically, evaluate expressions, and perform many operations that previously required a handheld graphing calculator.

**Strategic Implication:** Students taking the digital test should become proficient with Desmos well before test day. Knowing how to graph a system of equations, find intersection points, evaluate a function at a specific value, or use the table feature can save significant time. However, students should not rely on Desmos as a crutch—many problems are solved more quickly with algebraic reasoning, and the calculator should be used as a complement to mathematical understanding, not a replacement for it.

Students taking the paper-based version of the ACT will not have access to Desmos and must use an approved handheld calculator.

---

## 1.3 Legacy Format: Understanding the Historical Context

While the current standard is the enhanced 45-question format, some students may encounter references to the legacy 60-question format in older prep materials. Understanding this format remains useful for interpreting older practice tests and score conversions.

### Legacy Section Parameters

| Parameter | Specification |
|---|---|
| **Number of Questions** | 60 |
| **Time Allotted** | 60 minutes |
| **Answer Choices per Question** | 5 (A–E or F–K) |
| **Scoring Scale** | 1–36 (scaled score) |
| **Penalty for Wrong Answers** | None |
| **Calculator Policy** | Permitted for the entire section |
| **Reference Sheet** | None provided |

### Legacy Content Distribution

The legacy format organized questions into six content categories with predictable question counts:

| Category | Approximate # of Questions |
|---|---|
| Pre-Algebra | 14 |
| Elementary Algebra | 10 |
| Intermediate Algebra | 9 |
| Plane Geometry | 14 |
| Coordinate Geometry | 9 |
| Trigonometry | 4 |
| **Total** | **60** |

This distribution reveals that Plane Geometry and Pre-Algebra together comprised nearly 47% of the legacy test. Algebra-related categories (Elementary + Intermediate) accounted for about 32%. Trigonometry, while present, was a small slice—only 4 questions. This proportional weighting is critical: it tells us where the bulk of a student's preparation effort should be concentrated.

---

## 1.4 Current Content Categories and Reporting Categories

The enhanced ACT organizes its mathematical content into reporting categories that reflect the types of mathematical reasoning being assessed. These categories are used to generate the subscores that appear on the student's score report, and they provide a more nuanced picture of what the test measures.

### Preparing for Higher Math (~80% of scored questions)

This is the primary content area, encompassing the bulk of the test. It is subdivided into five categories:

#### Number & Quantity (~7%)

This category assesses understanding of the real and complex number systems, including:

- Properties of integers, rational numbers, and irrational numbers
- Integer and rational exponents, including negative and fractional exponents
- Vectors (addition, subtraction, scalar multiplication, magnitude)
- Matrices (addition, subtraction, multiplication, scalar multiplication, determinants of 2×2 matrices)
- Complex numbers (basic arithmetic, the definition that $i^2 = -1$)

**Strategic Note:** Number & Quantity questions are relatively few in number but represent foundational knowledge. A student who is shaky on exponent rules or matrix operations will lose points here that are essentially "free" with proper preparation. These questions tend to be straightforward and are excellent targets for quick points.

#### Algebra (~15%)

This category covers the manipulation and solution of equations and inequalities:

- Linear equations and inequalities in one variable
- Polynomial equations and inequalities
- Radical equations
- Exponential equations
- Systems of linear equations (two and three variables)
- Systems involving nonlinear equations
- Absolute value equations and inequalities

**Strategic Note:** Algebra is the single most heavily tested broad category on the ACT. Linear equations, systems of equations, and polynomial manipulation appear in a vast array of question types. Mastery of algebra is non-negotiable for any student targeting a score above 25.

#### Functions (~15%)

This category tests understanding of function concepts and specific function types:

- Function definition, notation, and evaluation
- Domain and range
- Linear functions
- Radical functions
- Piecewise functions
- Polynomial functions
- Logarithmic functions
- Graph transformations (shifts, reflections, stretches, compressions)
- Key features of graphs (intercepts, asymptotes, maxima, minima, symmetry)
- Composition of functions
- Inverse functions (conceptual understanding)

**Strategic Note:** The ACT loves function notation and graph transformations. A student must be comfortable reading $f(x)$, understanding what $f(a)$ means, and predicting how the graph of $f(x)$ changes when it becomes $f(x) + 3$, $f(x - 2)$, or $-f(x)$. These transformation concepts appear with high frequency and are highly preparable.

#### Geometry (~20%)

This category is the most heavily weighted and covers a broad range of geometric concepts:

- Congruence and similarity of geometric figures
- Area and perimeter of polygons
- Area and circumference of circles
- Volume and surface area of solids (prisms, cylinders, spheres, cones, pyramids)
- Trigonometric ratios in right triangles (sine, cosine, tangent)
- Properties of circles (central angles, arcs, sectors, chords, tangents)
- Properties of polygons (interior angles, exterior angles, diagonals)
- Conic sections (circles and parabolas primarily)
- Geometric proofs and logical reasoning
- Coordinate geometry (slope, distance, midpoint, equations of lines and circles)

**Strategic Note:** Geometry is the heavyweight champion of ACT Math. It encompasses both plane geometry (shapes, angles, area) and coordinate geometry (equations, graphs, transformations). Many geometry questions require the application of multiple formulas or concepts in a single problem, making them inherently more challenging than single-concept algebra questions. Students who are strong in geometry have a significant advantage.

#### Statistics & Probability (~10%)

This category assesses data analysis and probabilistic reasoning:

- Data interpretation from tables, charts, graphs, and scatterplots
- Measures of center (mean, median, mode)
- Measures of spread (range, interquartile range, standard deviation conceptually)
- Probability of single and compound events
- Combinations and permutations
- Sampling methods and experimental design
- Correlation and causation
- Normal distributions (conceptual)

**Strategic Note:** Statistics & Probability questions are often less about computation and more about interpretation and reasoning. A student needs to understand what a mean represents, how outliers affect it, what a margin of error implies, and how to calculate basic probabilities. These questions are often embedded in real-world contexts (surveys, experiments, data sets) and require careful reading.

### Integrating Essential Skills (~20% of scored questions)

This category captures the cross-cutting skills that appear throughout the test:

- Rates and proportional relationships
- Percentages (including percent change, successive percents, percent of a total)
- Area, surface area, and volume (applied in non-routine contexts)
- Average and median (in complex scenarios)
- Expressing numbers in different ways (fractions, decimals, percents, scientific notation)
- Multi-step, nonroutine problems that combine multiple skills from different categories

**Strategic Note:** "Integrating Essential Skills" is not a separate body of knowledge—it is a *style* of question. These are problems that require the student to combine concepts from two or more categories. For example, a question might require setting up a proportion (Pre-Algebra), solving a linear equation (Algebra), and interpreting the result in the context of a graph (Functions). These multi-step problems are where many students lose time and points, precisely because they require flexible thinking rather than rote application.

### Modeling (≥20% cross-coded)

Modeling questions are not a separate content category but are cross-coded with other categories. A modeling question is one that requires the student to:

- Produce a mathematical model (equation, graph, table) from a real-world scenario
- Interpret the features of a model in context
- Evaluate whether a model is appropriate for a given situation
- Improve or adjust a model based on new information

**Strategic Note:** Modeling questions are the ACT's way of testing applied mathematics. They often appear as word problems with tables, graphs, or verbal descriptions of real-world situations. The key skill is translation: converting English sentences into mathematical expressions and vice versa. Students should practice this translation skill extensively.

---

## 1.5 Question Difficulty Progression

One of the most consistent features of the ACT Math section is the general progression from easier to harder questions. While the exact difficulty ordering can vary slightly from test form to test form, the following pattern holds reliably:

### Questions 1–15: Easy

These questions test single concepts in straightforward contexts. They typically involve:

- Basic arithmetic operations
- Simple linear equations
- Basic percent calculations
- Reading a graph or table
- Applying a single formula (area, perimeter, circumference)
- Basic probability

**Time Budget:** Approximately 20–30 seconds per question. These questions should be answered quickly and confidently, banking time for harder problems later.

**Common Trap:** Overconfidence. Students who rush through easy questions often make careless errors. A question that asks for "the value of $x$" might actually ask for "the value of $3x$." Always verify what the question is asking for.

### Questions 16–30: Medium

These questions require either:

- Multiple steps within a single concept
- Application of a concept in a slightly unfamiliar context
- Combining two related concepts
- More complex algebraic manipulation
- Interpretation of more complex graphs or data

**Time Budget:** Approximately 45–75 seconds per question. These questions require careful reading and methodical work.

**Common Trap:** Setup errors. The ACT includes answer choices that result from common setup mistakes. For example, if a question asks for the area of a triangle, one answer choice might be the result of forgetting to divide by 2. Always double-check the formula being used.

### Questions 31–45: Hard

These questions are the most challenging and typically involve:

- Multiple concepts integrated in a single problem
- Unfamiliar or novel problem contexts
- Complex multi-step reasoning
- Abstract algebraic or function manipulation
- Advanced geometry (similarity, trigonometry, circle theorems)
- Problems that can be solved multiple ways but require insight to find the efficient path

**Time Budget:** Approximately 90–120 seconds per question. These questions demand the most time and mental energy.

**Common Trap:** Time mismanagement. Students who spend too long on early questions may not reach hard questions at the end, which is particularly damaging because the hard questions are worth the same points as easy ones. A student who gets questions 1–20 all correct but runs out of time on questions 40–45 is leaving significant points on the table.

---

## 1.6 Scoring Mechanics: From Raw Score to Scaled Score

Understanding how the ACT Math score is computed is essential for strategic test-taking. The scoring process involves two stages: the raw score and the scaled score.

### Raw Score

The raw score is simply the number of questions answered correctly. There is **no penalty for wrong answers**—an incorrect answer and a blank answer have the same effect (zero points for that question). This is one of the most strategically important facts about the ACT.

**Implication: Never leave a question blank.** Even a random guess has a 25% chance of being correct (on the 4-choice enhanced format) or a 20% chance (on the legacy 5-choice format). Over 45 questions, random guessing on 10 blanks would yield approximately 2.5 correct answers by chance alone. That could be the difference between a scaled score of 24 and 26.

### Scaled Score

The raw score is converted to a scaled score on a 1–36 scale using a conversion table that varies slightly from test form to test form. This conversion process (called "equating") ensures that scores from different test administrations are comparable—a 28 on the April test represents the same level of achievement as a 28 on the October test, even if the specific questions differ.

The exact conversion table is not published in advance and varies by test form, but approximate conversions for the legacy 60-question format provide a useful reference:

| Scaled Score | Approximate Questions Correct (Legacy 60Q) |
|---|---|
| 36 | 59–60 |
| 35 | 57–58 |
| 34 | 55–56 |
| 33 | 54 |
| 32 | 53 |
| 31 | 51–52 |
| 30 | 49–50 |
| 28 | 45–46 |
| 26 | 39–41 |
| 24 | 34–36 |
| 22 | 31 |
| 20 | 27–28 |
| 18 | 22–24 |
| 16 | 16–18 |
| 14 | 10–12 |
| 12 | 7 |
| 10 | 4 |
| 8 | 3 |
| 6 | 2 |
| 4 | 1 |
| 2–3 | 0 |

For the enhanced 45-question format, the conversion is proportionally adjusted. A student who gets 41 out of 45 questions correct (skipping 4) would likely receive a scaled score of 35 or 36, depending on the specific conversion table for that form.

### Score Benchmarks

Understanding where a student's target score falls on the scoring spectrum helps calibrate preparation strategy:

| Benchmark | Scaled Score | Approximate Percentile |
|---|---|---|
| National Average | ~19–20 | ~50th |
| Above Average | 22–24 | ~60th–70th |
| Competitive for Selective Colleges | 28–30 | ~90th–95th |
| Highly Competitive | 31–33 | ~96th–99th |
| Perfect/Near-Perfect | 34–36 | 99th+ |

**Strategic Note:** The scoring curve is relatively generous compared to many standardized tests. A student does not need a perfect raw score to receive a 36. On most forms, missing 1–2 questions still yields a 36. This means that a student who is strong in most content areas can afford to miss a few hard questions and still achieve an excellent score. This has implications for time management: it is better to ensure accuracy on all easy and medium questions (by working carefully and checking) than to rush through them in an attempt to reach hard questions.

### Subscores and Reporting Categories

In addition to the overall scaled score (1–36), the ACT Math section generates subscores for each reporting category. These subscores are also reported on a 1–36 scale and provide insight into a student's strengths and weaknesses within specific content areas. While most colleges focus primarily on the overall math scaled score and the composite score, the subscores are valuable diagnostic tools for students during their preparation.

---

## 1.7 The Composite Score and Its Implications

The ACT composite score is the rounded average of the four section scores (English, Math, Reading, and Science). It is reported on a 1–36 scale and is the single number that most colleges use for admissions decisions.

**Calculation:** $$\text{Composite} = \text{round}\left(\frac{\text{English} + \text{Math} + \text{Reading} + \text{Science}}{4}\right)$$

**Example:** A student scoring 28 English, 32 Math, 30 Reading, and 29 Science would have a composite of $\frac{28 + 32 + 30 + 29}{4} = \frac{119}{4} = 29.75$, which rounds to 30.

**Strategic Implication:** The math section contributes exactly 25% of the composite score. However, for many STEM-oriented programs, the math score is weighted more heavily in the admissions decision, either formally (some programs request section scores) or informally (admissions officers may note a particularly high or low math score). A student applying to an engineering program with a 34 math score but a 22 English score is in a very different position than a student with the reverse profile.

---

## 1.8 Calculator Policy: What Is and Is Not Permitted

The ACT calculator policy is specific and strictly enforced. Violations can result in score cancellation. Students must understand this policy thoroughly before test day.

### Permitted Calculators

- Four-function calculators (basic arithmetic)
- Scientific calculators (including those with trigonometric, logarithmic, and exponential functions)
- Most graphing calculators (TI-84 Plus, TI-84 Plus CE, TI-Nspire non-CAS, etc.)
- The built-in Desmos calculator (digital test only)

### NOT Permitted

- Calculators with Computer Algebra Systems (CAS) built in (TI-89, TI-92, TI-Nspire CAS, HP Prime, etc.)
- Calculators with QWERTY keyboards (typewriter-style key layouts)
- Calculators that require an electrical outlet
- Calculators that make noise
- Calculators with internet/communication capabilities (including smartphones, smartwatches, or tablets used as calculators)
- Calculators with a stylus or pen input
- Calculators with a CAS capability, even if the CAS features are not being used during the test

### Calculator Tips and Strategy

1. **Use the calculator strategically, not as a crutch.** Many ACT Math problems can be solved more quickly with mental math or algebraic reasoning than with a calculator. The calculator is most useful for:
   - Evaluating complex arithmetic (e.g., $3.14 \times 27^2$)
   - Checking work on problems solved algebraically
   - Graphing functions to find intercepts, intersections, or key features
   - Using the table feature to evaluate a function at multiple points

2. **Set up problems on paper first.** Before touching the calculator, write down the equation, expression, or operation you need to evaluate. This prevents order-of-operations errors and ensures you are calculating the right thing.

3. **Use parentheses liberally.** When entering expressions into a calculator, especially fractions, exponents, or square roots, use parentheses to ensure correct order of operations. For example, $\frac{3 + 5}{2 \times 4}$ should be entered as `(3+5)/(2*4)`, not as `3+5/2*4`.

4. **Be cautious with negative numbers.** Entering $-3^2$ into many calculators yields $-9$ (because the calculator interprets this as $-(3^2)$), not $9$ (which would be $(-3)^2$). Use parentheses: `(-3)^2` for a squared negative number.

5. **Store intermediate values.** If you calculate a value that will be used in a subsequent calculation, store it in the calculator's memory rather than re-entering it. This saves time and reduces the risk of transcription errors.

6. **Know your calculator.** Whatever calculator you plan to use on test day, practice with it extensively before the test. You should be able to perform basic operations, use trigonometric functions, graph equations, and use memory functions without looking at the keys. Fumbling with an unfamiliar calculator during the test wastes precious time.

7. **Bring fresh batteries or a backup calculator.** Calculator failure during the test is a preventable disaster. Bring a backup calculator or fresh batteries.

---

## 1.9 The No-Penalty Rule: Why You Must Answer Every Question

This point cannot be emphasized enough: **there is no penalty for wrong answers on the ACT.** This fundamentally changes the strategic calculus of test-taking compared to tests that penalize guessing (like the old SAT, which deducted 1/4 point for each wrong answer).

### The Mathematics of Guessing

On the enhanced ACT (4 answer choices per question):

- Probability of a correct random guess: $\frac{1}{4} = 25\%$
- Expected value of guessing on 10 questions: $10 \times 0.25 = 2.5$ additional correct answers
- Expected value of leaving 10 questions blank: $0$ additional correct answers

On the legacy ACT (5 answer choices per question):

- Probability of a correct random guess: $\frac{1}{5} = 20\%$
- Expected value of guessing on 10 questions: $10 \times 0.20 = 2$ additional correct answers
- Expected value of leaving 10 questions blank: $0$ additional correct answers

In both cases, guessing is mathematically advantageous compared to leaving questions blank. Over the course of an entire test section, strategic guessing can add 2–4 correct answers to a student's raw score, which can translate to 1–3 points on the scaled score.

### The "Guessing Letter" Strategy

When forced to guess randomly (because time is running out or a question is completely unknown), many test prep experts recommend choosing a consistent "guessing letter" (e.g., always guess B or always guess G). The logic is that since the ACT distributes correct answers approximately evenly across the four (or five) answer choices, consistently choosing the same letter ensures that you will hit the correct answer at the expected rate. If you guess randomly across different letters, you might (by chance) pick wrong answers more often than the statistical expectation.

**Important Caveat:** This strategy only applies to truly random guesses. If you can eliminate even one answer choice, your odds improve significantly:

- Eliminating 1 choice (3 remaining): $33.3\%$ chance of correct guess
- Eliminating 2 choices (2 remaining): $50\%$ chance of correct guess
- Eliminating 3 choices (1 remaining): $100\%$ chance of correct guess

The Process of Elimination (P.O.E.) is therefore one of the most powerful strategies available, and it is discussed in detail later in this chapter.

---

## 1.10 Time Management: The Central Strategic Challenge

Time management is arguably the single most important strategic skill on the ACT Math section. The test is designed so that many students will not finish, and the difference between a good score and a great score often comes down to how effectively a student allocates their limited time.

### The Time Crunch

On the enhanced ACT, students have 50 minutes for 45 questions, which averages to approximately 67 seconds per question. On the legacy format, it was 60 seconds per question. However, not all questions require the same amount of time. The key insight is that **all questions are worth the same number of points** (1 raw point each), regardless of their difficulty. A hard question that takes 3 minutes is far less "valuable" per unit of time than three easy questions that each take 30 seconds.

### Recommended Time Budget (Enhanced 45-Question Format)

| Question Range | Difficulty | Time Per Question | Cumulative Time |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1–15 | Easy | ~25–30 seconds | ~6–7.5 minutes |
| 16–30 | Medium | ~45–60 seconds | ~17–22 minutes |
| 31–45 | Hard | ~75–100 seconds | ~28–35 minutes |
| Review | — | Remaining time | ~5–10 minutes |

**Total: 50 minutes**

The goal is to move through the easy questions quickly (but carefully), spend appropriate time on medium questions, and allocate the remaining time to hard questions while leaving a few minutes at the end to review flagged questions and ensure no bubbles are left blank.

### The "Two-Pass" Strategy

Many high-scoring students employ a two-pass approach:

**Pass 1 (First ~35–40 minutes):** Go through the section from question 1 to the end, answering every question that can be solved in under 90 seconds. For questions that seem time-consuming or confusing, mark them and move on immediately. The goal is to reach the end of the section, answering all the "quick" questions and banking maximum points.

**Pass 2 (Final ~10–15 minutes):** Return to the marked questions and work on them with the remaining time. Since these are the hardest questions, having the full remaining time to focus on them (rather than rushing past them in sequence) often leads to better results.

**Why This Works:** The two-pass strategy ensures that a student never leaves an easy question unanswered because they spent too long on a hard question earlier in the section. It also provides a psychological benefit: the student feels a sense of momentum and accomplishment as they move through the section quickly on the first pass, rather than feeling bogged down by hard questions early on.

### The "Flag and Move" Rule

If a student has spent more than 60 seconds on a question and has not made clear progress toward a solution, they should flag the question and move on. This is not giving up—it is strategic resource allocation. The 60-second threshold is a guideline, not a rigid rule, but it prevents the most common time management disaster: spending 3–4 minutes on a single hard question and never reaching 5–10 easier questions at the end of the section.

### Pacing Checkpoints

Students should be aware of where they should be at various points during the section:

| Time Elapsed | Target Question (Enhanced) |
|---|---|
| 10 minutes | Question 18–22 |
| 20 minutes | Question 30–34 |
| 30 minutes | Question 38–42 |
| 40 minutes | Question 45 (finished or nearly finished) |
| 45 minutes | Reviewing flagged questions |
| 50 minutes | All bubbles filled |

If a student finds themselves significantly behind these checkpoints, they need to pick up the pace—even if it means making educated guesses on a few questions rather than solving them fully.

---

## 1.11 Core Problem-Solving Strategies

Beyond time management, the ACT Math section rewards students who employ specific problem-solving strategies. These are not "tricks"—they are legitimate mathematical approaches that the test designers expect knowledgeable students to use.

### Strategy 1: Backsolving (Plugging in Answer Choices)

**Concept:** Instead of solving an equation algebraically, substitute the answer choices into the problem and see which one works.

**When to Use It:**
- When the answer choices are specific numbers (integers, simple fractions)
- When the algebraic setup of the problem is complex or time-consuming
- When the question asks for a specific value of a variable

**How to Do It:**
1. Start by testing answer choice **B** or **C** (the middle values). This allows you to eliminate multiple choices based on whether the result is too high or too low.
2. If the result is too high, eliminate all choices larger than the one you tested.
3. If the result is too low, eliminate all choices smaller than the one you tested.
4. Continue testing until you find the correct answer.

**Example Context:** A word problem that results in an equation like $3(2x + 5) - 4(x - 3) = 47$. Rather than solving algebraically, a student could plug in the answer choices for $x$ and see which one makes the equation true.

**Efficiency Tip:** Starting with the middle value (B or C) is optimal because it allows you to eliminate half the choices in one step, regardless of whether the result is too high or too low.

### Strategy 2: Picking Numbers

**Concept:** When a problem contains variables in both the question and the answer choices, substitute simple numbers for the variables to make the problem concrete.

**When to Use It:**
- When answer choices contain variables or algebraic expressions
- When the problem describes a general relationship (e.g., "If $x$ is increased by 20%...")
- When the problem involves abstract percent changes or proportional relationships

**How to Do It:**
1. Choose simple numbers for the variables (good choices: 2, 3, 5, 10, 100). Avoid 0 and 1, as they can produce ambiguous results.
2. Solve the problem using your chosen numbers, obtaining a specific numerical answer.
3. Substitute your chosen numbers into each answer choice and see which one matches your result.

**Example Context:** "If the length of a rectangle is increased by 25% and the width is decreased by 20%, by what percent does the area change?" Pick length = 100 and width = 100. New length = 125, new width = 80. New area = 10,000. Original area = 10,000. Percent change = 0%. Now plug into answer choices to find which one gives 0%.

**Efficiency Tip:** If two answer choices match your result when you pick one set of numbers, pick a different set of numbers and test again. This is rare but can happen.

### Strategy 3: Process of Elimination (P.O.E.)

**Concept:** Rather than solving for the correct answer directly, eliminate answer choices that are clearly wrong.

**When to Use It:**
- Always. P.O.E. should be a constant companion on every question.
- Especially useful when the exact solution is difficult to compute but obviously wrong answers are easy to identify.

**How to Do It:**
1. **Sign errors:** If the answer must be positive (e.g., a distance, a length), eliminate negative choices.
2. **Magnitude errors:** If the answer should be approximately 50, eliminate choices like 5,000 or 0.005.
3. **Parity errors:** If the answer must be even (e.g., an even integer), eliminate odd choices.
4. **Range errors:** If the answer must be between 0 and 1 (e.g., a probability), eliminate choices greater than 1.
5. **Algebraic errors:** If setting up an equation, check whether each answer choice satisfies the conditions of the problem.

**Efficiency Tip:** Even eliminating one or two choices before guessing significantly improves the expected value of a guess. Eliminating one choice on every question (from 4 options to 3) increases the expected number of correct guesses from 25% to 33.3%.

### Strategy 4: Estimation and Approximation

**Concept:** When answer choices are spread far apart, approximate calculations rather than computing exact values.

**When to Use It:**
- When answer choices differ by large amounts (e.g., 5, 50, 500, 5000)
- When the problem involves messy numbers (e.g., $\pi \approx 3.14$, $\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414$)
- When the question asks for an approximate value

**How to Do It:**
1. Round numbers to convenient values (e.g., $\pi \approx 3$, $\sqrt{50} \approx 7$)
2. Perform the calculation with rounded numbers
3. Select the answer choice closest to your approximation

**Example Context:** "What is the area of a circle with radius 7.9?" Approximate radius as 8. Area = $\pi r^2 \approx 3.14 \times 64 \approx 201$. The answer choice closest to 201 is correct.

### Strategy 5: Drawing and Diagramming

**Concept:** For geometry problems, word problems, and any question involving spatial relationships, draw a diagram or create a table.

**When to Use It:**
- Geometry problems (especially when no figure is provided)
- Word problems involving multiple quantities or relationships
- Problems involving motion, distance, or rate
- Problems involving overlapping sets (Venn diagrams)

**How to Do It:**
1. Sketch the situation on scratch paper
2. Label all known quantities
3. Identify relationships between quantities
4. Use the diagram to set up equations or identify geometric relationships

**Efficiency Tip:** Even when a figure is provided, redraw it if it is small or unclear. Adding annotations (marking equal sides, right angles, known values) can reveal relationships that are not immediately obvious.

### Strategy 6: Keyword Identification

**Concept:** Identify keywords in word problems that indicate mathematical operations or relationships.

**Key Translations:**

| English Phrase | Mathematical Meaning |
|---|---|
| "is" | $=$ |
| "more than" | addition |
| "less than" | subtraction |
| "of" | multiplication |
| "per" or "each" | multiplication or division |
| "at least" | $\ge$ |
| "at most" or "no more than" | $\le$ |
| "more than" (in inequalities) | $>$ |
| "less than" (in inequalities) | $<$ |
| "a function of" | indicates the dependent variable |
| "ratio of A to B" | $\frac{A}{B}$ |
| "percent" | $\div 100$ |
| "congruent" | equal in measure (angles) or length (sides) |
| "distinct" | different values |

**Efficiency Tip:** Underline or circle keywords as you read. This prevents misreading, which is one of the most common sources of errors on the ACT Math section.

---

## 1.12 Common Mistakes and Traps

The ACT Math section is designed with specific traps that exploit common student errors. Awareness of these traps is the first step in avoiding them.

### Trap 1: Solving for the Wrong Variable

**The Trap:** A question asks for the value of $3x$, but the student solves for $x$ and selects that value as the answer. The answer choices include both the value of $x$ and the value of $3x$.

**The Defense:** Before selecting an answer, re-read the last sentence of the question. Circle what the question is actually asking for. If you solve for $x$ but the question asks for $3x$, multiply your answer by 3 before selecting.

### Trap 2: Misapplying Formulas

**The Trap:** The student uses the wrong formula—for example, using the circumference formula ($C = 2\pi r$) when the question asks for area ($A = \pi r^2$), or using the volume of a rectangular box ($V = lwh$) when the question involves a cone ($V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$).

**The Defense:** Write down the formula before plugging in numbers. Verify that the formula matches the shape or relationship described in the problem. If unsure, sketch the situation and label the relevant dimensions.

### Trap 3: Order of Operations Errors

**The Trap:** The student evaluates $3 + 4 \times 5$ as $(3 + 4) \times 5 = 35$ instead of $3 + (4 \times 5) = 23$. Or the student enters an expression into the calculator without proper parentheses.

**The Defense:** Always follow PEMDAS (Parentheses, Exponents, Multiplication/Division, Addition/Subtraction). When using a calculator, use parentheses around numerators, denominators, and exponents.

### Trap 4: Negative Number Errors

**The Trap:** The student makes an error when working with negative numbers—for example, $(-3)^2 = -9$ (incorrect; the correct answer is 9), or $-3 - (-5) = -8$ (incorrect; the correct answer is 2).

**The Defense:** When working with negative numbers, write out each step. Use parentheses to clarify operations. Double-check any calculation involving negatives.

### Trap 5: Misreading the Question

**The Trap:** The student reads "Which of the following is NOT a solution..." and proceeds to find the solution. Or the student reads "the area of the triangle" as "the perimeter of the triangle."

**The Defense:** Underline the key word (NOT, EXCEPT, NOT, etc.) and the specific quantity being asked for. Read the question twice before solving.

### Trap 6: Over-Reliance on the Calculator

**The Trap:** The student uses the calculator for every calculation, including simple arithmetic that could be done mentally. This wastes time and can lead to errors (e.g., entering a number incorrectly).

**The Defense:** Use mental math for simple calculations. Reserve the calculator for complex arithmetic, graphing, and checking work.

### Trap 7: Spending Too Long on One Question

**The Trap:** The student encounters a hard question, becomes determined to solve it, and spends 3–4 minutes on it—time that could have been spent answering 3–4 easier questions later in the section.

**The Defense:** Apply the 60-second rule. If no clear progress has been made in 60 seconds, flag the question and move on. Return to it if time permits.

### Trap 8: Filling in the Wrong Bubble

**The Trap:** The student solves question 12 but accidentally fills in the bubble for answer 13. This can cascade, causing every subsequent answer to be misaligned.

**The Defense:** After solving each question, verify that you are filling in the correct bubble. Some students use a "finger check"—pointing to the question number with one finger and the answer bubble with the other.

### Trap 9: Forgetting to Convert Units

**The Trap:** The problem gives measurements in different units (e.g., feet and inches, meters and centimeters) and the student fails to convert before calculating.

**The Defense:** When a problem involves measurements, check all units. If they are different, convert to a common unit before performing calculations. Write the conversion factor on paper.

### Trap 10: Assuming Figures Are Drawn to Scale

**The Trap:** The ACT's standard assumptions explicitly state that figures are NOT necessarily drawn to scale. A student might assume an angle is 90° because it looks like a right angle, or assume two segments are equal because they appear equal in length.

**The Defense:** Never assume measurements or relationships based on how a figure looks. Only use information that is explicitly given (labeled angles, marked congruent sides, stated relationships) or that can be proven from given information.

---

## 1.13 The ACT Math Score Report: Understanding Your Results

After taking the ACT, students receive a score report that provides detailed information about their performance. Understanding this report is essential for targeted improvement.

### Overall Math Score (1–36)

This is the primary score that colleges see. It represents the student's overall mathematical achievement on the ACT.

### Reporting Category Scores

The score report breaks down performance into the reporting categories described earlier:

- Number & Quantity
- Algebra
- Functions
- Geometry
- Statistics & Probability
- Integrating Essential Skills
- Modeling

Each category is reported as a scaled score (1–36) and/or a percentile rank. These subscores reveal a student's relative strengths and weaknesses. For example, a student might score 32 in Algebra but 24 in Geometry, indicating that geometry is an area for improvement.

### STEM Score

The ACT also reports a STEM (Science, Technology, Engineering, and Mathematics) score, which is the average of the Math and Science section scores. For students interested in STEM programs, this score is particularly relevant.

### Score Ranks

The score report includes national percentile ranks, showing how the student's score compares to other test-takers. For example, a math score of 28 might correspond to the 92nd percentile, meaning the student scored higher than 92% of test-takers.

---

## 1.14 ACT vs. SAT Math: Key Differences

While this guide focuses exclusively on the ACT, understanding how it compares to the SAT Math section can provide useful context, especially for students who are deciding which test to take.

| Feature | ACT Math | SAT Math |
|---|---|---|
| **Calculator Policy** | Calculator permitted on entire section | Calculator permitted on one of two modules; no-calculator module exists |
| **Formula Reference Sheet** | None provided | Reference sheet with common formulas provided |
| **Number of Questions** | 45 (enhanced) / 60 (legacy) | 44 (digital) / 58 (paper) |
| **Time** | 50 minutes (enhanced) / 60 minutes (legacy) | 70 minutes (digital, two 35-min modules) |
| **Answer Choices** | 4 (enhanced) / 5 (legacy) | 4 |
| **Content Emphasis** | More geometry and trigonometry | More algebra and data analysis |
| **Question Style** | More straightforward wording | More complex, multi-step problem solving |
| **Trigonometry** | Present (4 questions on legacy) | Minimal or absent |
| **Score Weight** | 1 of 4 sections (25% of composite) | 2 of 2 sections (50% of total score) |

**Strategic Implication:** Students who are strong in geometry and comfortable with a faster pace may prefer the ACT. Students who are strong in algebra and data analysis, or who benefit from having a formula sheet and a no-calculator section that tests mental math, may prefer the SAT. Many students take both tests and submit the one on which they score higher.

---

## 1.15 Preparation Timeline and Philosophy

While detailed preparation strategies are covered in subsequent chapters, a brief overview of the preparation philosophy is appropriate here.

### When to Begin

Students should begin ACT Math preparation at least **3 months** before their target test date. This allows sufficient time to:

1. Take a diagnostic practice test to identify baseline performance and weaknesses
2. Review and learn content areas that are weak
3. Practice problem-solving strategies under timed conditions
4. Take multiple full-length practice tests to build stamina and refine time management
5. Review missed questions and identify error patterns

### How Much Time to Dedicate

Research and experience suggest the following approximate relationships between study time and score improvement:

| Weekly Study Hours | Duration | Expected Improvement |
|---|---|---|
| 5 hours | 3 months | 2–4 points |
| 10 hours | 3 months | 4–6 points |
| 15–20 hours | 3 months | 6–9+ points |

These are rough estimates and vary based on the student's starting point, the quality of their preparation, and their ability to learn from mistakes.

### The Quality vs. Quantity Principle

Not all study time is equally effective. The most productive preparation activities, in approximate order of impact, are:

1. **Reviewing missed questions** on practice tests (identifying and understanding errors)
2. **Targeted content review** in weak areas
3. **Timed practice tests** (full-length, under realistic conditions)
4. **Strategy practice** (backsolving, P.O.E., estimation)
5. **Untimed practice problems** (for content learning)

Simply doing hundreds of practice problems without reviewing mistakes is far less effective than doing fewer problems but thoroughly analyzing every error.

---

## 1.16 Test Day Logistics and Mindset

### What to Bring

- **Admission ticket** (printed or on phone)
- **Photo ID** (government-issued or school ID)
- **Approved calculator** (with fresh batteries)
- **Backup calculator** (optional but recommended)
- **Pencils** (#2, sharpened—not mechanical pencils)
- **Eraser**
- **Watch** (non-smart, non-digital-display if prohibited—check current policy)
- **Snack and water** (for the break after the Math section)

### What NOT to Bring

- Phone (must be turned off or silenced and stored away)
- Notes or study materials (not permitted in the testing room)
- Scratch paper (provided by the test center)
- Prohibited calculators

### Mindset

- **Confidence:** The ACT Math section is predictable. The same concepts, question types, and strategies appear on every test. A well-prepared student should feel confident that they have the tools to handle any question.
- **Calm:** Anxiety impairs working memory and leads to careless errors. Deep breathing, positive self-talk, and familiarity with the test format (gained through practice tests) all help manage anxiety.
- **Focus:** During the section, focus only on the current question. Do not dwell on a previous question or worry about a future one. Each question is an independent opportunity to earn a point.
- **Discipline:** Stick to the time management plan. If a question is taking too long, flag it and move on. Trust the process.

---

## 1.17 Summary of Key Facts

This section consolidates the most critical facts about the ACT Math section for quick reference:

| Fact | Detail |
|---|---|
| **Questions** | 45 (enhanced) |
| **Time** | 50 minutes |
| **Answer Choices** | 4 per question |
| **Scoring** | 1–36 scaled score |
| **Penalty for Wrong Answers** | None |
| **Calculator** | Permitted (entire section) |
| **Formula Sheet** | Not provided |
| **Question Order** | Generally increasing difficulty |
| **Field Test Questions** | ~4 unscored, unidentifiable |
| **Content Categories** | Number & Quantity, Algebra, Functions, Geometry, Statistics & Probability |
| **Heaviest Content Areas** | Geometry (~20%), Algebra (~15%), Functions (~15%) |
| **Composite Weight** | 25% of overall ACT composite |
| **National Average** | ~19–20 |
| **Perfect Score** | 36 (can miss ~1–2 questions) |

---

## 1.18 Chapter Conclusion

The ACT Math section is a predictable, learnable test. It does not test genius-level mathematical ability—it tests the application of high school mathematics concepts under time pressure. A student who understands the test's architecture, masters the core content areas, employs effective problem-solving strategies, manages time wisely, and reviews errors systematically will score well above the national average.

The chapters that follow will dive deep into each content area, providing comprehensive review of every concept tested on the ACT Math section, along with specific strategies for each question type. But everything begins with the blueprint established in this chapter: knowing what you're facing, how it's scored, and how to approach it strategically.

The test is not an obstacle. It is a puzzle. And every puzzle has a solution.

---

---


# Chapter 2: Core Formula Bible — Every Equation and Identity You Must Memorize

---

## 2.0 — Why This Chapter Exists and How to Use It

The ACT Mathematics section provides **no formula reference sheet**. Every single equation, identity, and relationship you need must come from your own memory. This is one of the most significant structural differences between the ACT and the SAT, and it means that memorization is not optional — it is a prerequisite for a competitive score.

This chapter is designed to be the single most comprehensive, exhaustive, and deeply explained collection of every formula, identity, and mathematical relationship that has appeared on the ACT Mathematics section, organized by domain. For each formula, you will find:

- The formula itself, written in standard mathematical notation
- A verbal explanation of what each variable represents
- The conditions under which the formula applies
- Common variations and rearrangements
- Traps and misconceptions that the ACT exploits
- Connections to other formulas in this chapter

Treat this chapter as a living document. You should return to it repeatedly throughout your preparation. By test day, every formula here should be retrievable from memory in under two seconds.

---

## 2.1 — Coordinate Geometry Formulas

Coordinate geometry — the study of geometric figures on the Cartesian plane using algebraic equations — is one of the most heavily tested domains on the ACT. The following formulas are absolutely essential.

---

### 2.1.1 — Slope Formula

$$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$$

**What it means:** The slope $m$ of a line passing through two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is the ratio of the vertical change (rise) to the horizontal change (run) between those points.

**Critical details:**

- The order of subtraction matters for consistency. If you subtract $y_1$ from $y_2$ in the numerator, you must subtract $x_1$ from $x_2$ in the denominator. Reversing only one of these will give you the wrong sign.
- A **positive** slope means the line rises as it moves from left to right.
- A **negative** slope means the line falls as it moves from left to right.
- A slope of **zero** means the line is horizontal (of the form $y = k$).
- An **undefined** slope (division by zero) means the line is vertical (of the form $x = k$).
- Slope is the **rate of change** of $y$ with respect to $x$. In any real-world linear model, the slope tells you how much the dependent variable changes for each one-unit increase in the independent variable.

**ACT trap:** The test may give you two points and ask for the slope, but the points may involve fractions or negative numbers. Be meticulous with signs. A very common wrong answer choice is the result of forgetting a negative sign.

**Fractional slope interpretation:** If $m = \frac{3}{4}$, this means that for every 4 units the line moves to the right, it rises by 3 units. Equivalently, for every 1 unit the line moves to the right, it rises by $\frac{3}{4}$ of a unit.

---

### 2.1.2 — Slope-Intercept Form

$$y = mx + b$$

**What it means:** This is the most useful form of a linear equation for graphing purposes. The variable $m$ represents the slope of the line, and $b$ represents the $y$-intercept (the $y$-coordinate of the point where the line crosses the $y$-axis, i.e., where $x = 0$).

**Critical details:**

- When an equation is already in this form, you can immediately read off the slope and $y$-intercept without any computation.
- If the equation is given in another form (e.g., $3x + 2y = 6$), you must rearrange it into slope-intercept form to identify the slope and $y$-intercept.
- The $y$-intercept $b$ is always the point $(0, b)$.

**Example of rearrangement:** Given $3x + 2y = 6$, subtract $3x$ from both sides to get $2y = -3x + 6$, then divide by 2 to get $y = -\frac{3}{2}x + 3$. The slope is $-\frac{3}{2}$ and the $y$-intercept is $(0, 3)$.

---

### 2.1.3 — Point-Slope Form

$$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$$

**What it means:** This form is used when you know the slope $m$ of a line and one point $(x_1, y_1)$ that lies on the line. It is the most efficient form for constructing the equation of a line given a point and a slope.

**Critical details:**

- The subscript values $x_1$ and $y_1$ are the coordinates of the known point. They are not variables — they are specific numbers.
- After writing the equation in point-slope form, you may need to rearrange it into slope-intercept or standard form, depending on what the question asks for.
- This form is derived directly from the slope formula. If $(x, y)$ is any point on the line and $(x_1, y_1)$ is a known point, then $m = \frac{y - y_1}{x - x_1}$. Multiplying both sides by $(x - x_1)$ gives the point-slope form.

---

### 2.1.4 — Standard Form

$$Ax + By = C$$

**What it means:** In this form, $A$, $B$, and $C$ are integers (whole numbers), and by convention $A$ should be positive. This form is particularly useful for systems of equations and for certain types of word problems.

**Critical details:**

- To find the slope from standard form, rearrange into slope-intercept form. The slope is $-\frac{A}{B}$.
- The $y$-intercept is $\frac{C}{B}$ (found by setting $x = 0$).
- The $x$-intercept is $\frac{C}{A}$ (found by setting $y = 0$).
- When the ACT asks for the equation of a line in standard form, make sure $A$, $B$, and $C$ are integers with no common factor, and that $A$ is positive.

**ACT trap:** A common wrong answer choice will have the correct values but with $A$ negative, or with fractional coefficients instead of integers.

---

### 2.1.5 — Midpoint Formula

$$\left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

**What it means:** The midpoint of a line segment with endpoints $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is found by averaging the $x$-coordinates and averaging the $y$-coordinates.

**Critical details:**

- The midpoint is the point exactly halfway between the two endpoints.
- This formula works in any number of dimensions. In three dimensions, you would also average the $z$-coordinates.
- The midpoint formula is essentially the arithmetic mean applied to each coordinate independently.

**Conceptual understanding:** If you think of the two endpoints as positions on a number line, the midpoint is simply the average of the two positions. This is intuitive: the halfway point between 2 and 8 is $(2+8)/2 = 5$.

---

### 2.1.6 — Distance Formula

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

**What it means:** The distance $d$ between two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ in the Cartesian plane is given by this formula, which is a direct application of the Pythagorean Theorem.

**Critical details:**

- The distance formula is derived by constructing a right triangle where the segment connecting the two points is the hypotenuse. The horizontal leg has length $|x_2 - x_1|$ and the vertical leg has length $|y_2 - y_1|$. By the Pythagorean Theorem, the hypotenuse squared equals the sum of the squares of the legs.
- Because the differences are squared, the order of subtraction does not matter: $(x_2 - x_1)^2 = (x_1 - x_2)^2$.
- The result is always non-negative (distance cannot be negative).
- This formula can be extended to three dimensions: $d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2 + (z_2 - z_1)^2}$.

**ACT trap:** The test may ask for the distance between two points and include a wrong answer choice that is the sum of the absolute differences (the "taxicab" or Manhattan distance) rather than the Euclidean distance. Always use the square root of the sum of squared differences.

---

### 2.1.7 — Parallel and Perpendicular Line Conditions

**Parallel Lines:** Two lines are parallel if and only if they have the **same slope**.

$$m_1 = m_2$$

**Perpendicular Lines:** Two lines are perpendicular if and only if their slopes are **negative reciprocals** of each other.

$$m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1 \quad \text{or equivalently} \quad m_2 = -\frac{1}{m_1}$$

**Critical details:**

- A horizontal line (slope = 0) is perpendicular to a vertical line (undefined slope). This is a special case that does not fit the negative reciprocal formula because division by zero is undefined, but it is geometrically true.
- If a line has slope $\frac{3}{4}$, a line perpendicular to it has slope $-\frac{4}{3}$.
- If a line has slope $-5$, a line perpendicular to it has slope $\frac{1}{5}$.
- Parallel lines never intersect (unless they are the same line). Perpendicular lines intersect at a $90^\circ$ angle.

**ACT trap:** The test may give you the equation of a line and ask for the equation of a parallel or perpendicular line passing through a specific point. A very common error is to use the correct slope but forget to use the point-slope form with the new point, instead reusing the $y$-intercept from the original line.

---

### 2.1.8 — Equation of a Circle (Standard Form)

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

**What it means:** This is the equation of a circle with center at $(h, k)$ and radius $r$.

**Critical details:**

- The center is at $(h, k)$, **not** at $(-h, -k)$. The signs in the equation are subtracted, so if the equation is $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 5)^2 = 49$, the center is at $(3, -5)$ and the radius is $7$.
- The radius $r$ is always positive. If the right side of the equation is $r^2$, you must take the square root to find $r$.
- If the equation is given in expanded form (e.g., $x^2 + y^2 - 6x + 10y + 18 = 0$), you must complete the square for both $x$ and $y$ to convert it to standard form and identify the center and radius.

**Completing the square — quick reference:** For an expression like $x^2 + bx$, add and subtract $\left(\frac{b}{2}\right)^2$ to create a perfect square trinomial. For example, $x^2 - 6x$ becomes $(x - 3)^2 - 9$.

**ACT trap:** The most common mistake with circle equations is misidentifying the center coordinates due to sign errors. Remember: $(x - h)$ means the $x$-coordinate of the center is $h$, and $(y - k)$ means the $y$-coordinate of the center is $k$.

---

## 2.2 — Plane Geometry Formulas

Plane geometry — the study of two-dimensional shapes — constitutes approximately 20% of the ACT Mathematics section. The following formulas are non-negotiable.

---

### 2.2.1 — Angle Relationships

**Triangle Sum Theorem:** The sum of the interior angles of any triangle is $180^\circ$.

$$\angle A + \angle B + \angle C = 180^\circ$$

**Sum of Interior Angles of a Polygon:** For any polygon with $n$ sides, the sum of the interior angles is:

$$(n - 2) \times 180^\circ$$

**Critical details:**

- For a quadrilateral ($n = 4$): $(4 - 2) \times 180^\circ = 360^\circ$.
- For a pentagon ($n = 5$): $(5 - 2) \times 180^\circ = 540^\circ$.
- For a hexagon ($n = 6$): $(6 - 2) \times 180^\circ = 720^\circ$.
- For a regular polygon (all sides and angles equal), each interior angle measures $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$.

**Exterior Angles:** The sum of the exterior angles of **any** convex polygon, one at each vertex, is always $360^\circ$, regardless of the number of sides.

**Complementary Angles:** Two angles whose measures sum to $90^\circ$.

**Supplementary Angles:** Two angles whose measures sum to $180^\circ$.

**Vertical Angles:** When two lines intersect, the opposite (vertical) angles are always congruent.

**Parallel Lines Cut by a Transversal:** When a transversal intersects two parallel lines, corresponding angles are congruent, alternate interior angles are congruent, and same-side interior angles are supplementary.

---

### 2.2.2 — Perimeter Formulas

**Rectangle:** $P = 2l + 2w$ (where $l$ = length, $w$ = width)

**Square:** $P = 4s$ (where $s$ = side length)

**Triangle:** $P = a + b + c$ (sum of all three sides)

**Regular Polygon:** $P = n \cdot s$ (where $n$ = number of sides, $s$ = side length)

**Circle (Circumference):** $C = 2\pi r$ or $C = \pi d$ (where $r$ = radius, $d$ = diameter)

---

### 2.2.3 — Area Formulas

**Rectangle:** $A = lw$

**Square:** $A = s^2$

**Parallelogram:** $A = bh$ (base times height — the height must be perpendicular to the base)

**Triangle:** $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$

**Equilateral Triangle:** $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$ (where $s$ = side length)

**Trapezoid:** $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ (average of the two bases times the height)

**Circle:** $A = \pi r^2$

**Rhombus (using diagonals):** $A = \frac{1}{2}d_1 d_2$ (where $d_1$ and $d_2$ are the lengths of the diagonals)

**Sector of a Circle:** $A = \pi r^2 \cdot \frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ (where $\theta$ is the central angle in degrees)

**Critical details on area:**

- For a triangle, the height must be measured perpendicular to the base. In a non-right triangle, the height may fall outside the triangle.
- For a parallelogram, the height is not the length of a slanted side — it is the perpendicular distance between the bases.
- The area of a trapezoid formula can be understood as the average of the two parallel bases multiplied by the height. This is analogous to finding the average width and multiplying by the height.

**Heron's Formula (for a triangle given all three sides):**

$$A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$$

where $s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$ is the semi-perimeter.

**Critical details on Heron's Formula:** This formula is useful when you know all three side lengths of a triangle but do not know the height. The ACT occasionally tests this, though it is less common than the standard $\frac{1}{2}bh$ formula.

---

### 2.2.4 — Arc Length and Sector Area

**Arc Length:** The length of an arc subtended by a central angle $\theta$ (in degrees) in a circle of radius $r$ is:

$$L = 2\pi r \cdot \frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$$

**Sector Area:** The area of a sector with central angle $\theta$ (in degrees) in a circle of radius $r$ is:

$$A = \pi r^2 \cdot \frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$$

**Critical details:**

- Both formulas are simply the fraction $\frac{\theta}{360^\circ}$ of the whole circle's circumference or area.
- If $\theta = 90^\circ$, the arc length is $\frac{1}{4}$ of the circumference and the sector area is $\frac{1}{4}$ of the circle's area.
- If the central angle is given in radians, the arc length formula becomes $L = r\theta$ and the sector area becomes $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$.

---

### 2.2.5 — Pythagorean Theorem

$$a^2 + b^2 = c^2$$

**What it means:** In a right triangle, the square of the hypotenuse ($c$, the side opposite the right angle) equals the sum of the squares of the other two sides ($a$ and $b$).

**Critical details:**

- This theorem applies **only** to right triangles.
- The hypotenuse is always the longest side.
- If you know any two sides of a right triangle, you can find the third side.
- To find the hypotenuse: $c = \sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$.
- To find a leg: $a = \sqrt{c^2 - b^2}$.

**Pythagorean Triples (must be memorized):**

| Triple | Multiples |
|---|---|
| 3, 4, 5 | 6, 8, 10; 9, 12, 15; 12, 16, 20; etc. |
| 5, 12, 13 | 10, 24, 26; etc. |
| 7, 24, 25 | 14, 48, 50; etc. |
| 8, 15, 17 | 16, 30, 34; etc. |

**Why these matter:** The ACT frequently constructs right triangles with these side lengths. If you recognize a Pythagorean triple instantly, you can save significant time. For example, if a right triangle has legs of length 6 and 8, you should immediately know the hypotenuse is 10 — no calculator needed.

**Converse of the Pythagorean Theorem:** If $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$ for a triangle with sides $a$, $b$, and $c$, then the triangle is a right triangle. This is useful for determining whether a given triangle is right, acute, or obtuse:

- If $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$: right triangle
- If $a^2 + b^2 > c^2$: acute triangle (all angles $< 90^\circ$)
- If $a^2 + b^2 < c^2$: obtuse triangle (one angle $> 90^\circ$)

---

### 2.2.6 — Special Right Triangles

#### 45°-45°-90° Triangle

**Side ratio:** $s : s : s\sqrt{2}$

The two legs are congruent, and the hypotenuse is $\sqrt{2}$ times the length of each leg.

**Derivation:** A 45°-45°-90° triangle is half of a square cut along its diagonal. If the square has side length $s$, the diagonal is $s\sqrt{2}$ by the Pythagorean Theorem.

**Practical use:** If a 45°-45°-90° triangle has legs of length 7, the hypotenuse is $7\sqrt{2}$. If the hypotenuse is $10$, each leg is $\frac{10}{\sqrt{2}} = 5\sqrt{2}$ (after rationalizing the denominator).

#### 30°-60°-90° Triangle

**Side ratio:** $s : s\sqrt{3} : 2s$

The side opposite the 30° angle is the shortest ($s$), the side opposite the 60° angle is $s\sqrt{3}$, and the hypotenuse (opposite the 90° angle) is $2s$.

**Derivation:** A 30°-60°-90° triangle is half of an equilateral triangle cut along an altitude. If the equilateral triangle has side length $2s$, the altitude divides it into two 30°-60°-90° triangles with hypotenuse $2s$, short leg $s$, and long leg $s\sqrt{3}$ (found via the Pythagorean Theorem).

**Practical use:** If the short leg of a 30°-60°-90° triangle is 5, the long leg is $5\sqrt{3}$ and the hypotenuse is 10. If the hypotenuse is 14, the short leg is 7 and the long leg is $7\sqrt{3}$.

**ACT trap:** Students frequently confuse which leg gets the $\sqrt{3}$. Remember: the **longer** leg (opposite the larger 60° angle) gets the $\sqrt{3}$. The shorter leg (opposite the smaller 30° angle) does not.

---

### 2.2.7 — Triangle Inequality Theorem

For any triangle with side lengths $a$, $b$, and $c$:

$$|a - b| < c < a + b$$

**What it means:** The length of any side of a triangle must be **greater than the positive difference** of the other two sides and **less than the sum** of the other two sides.

**Critical details:**

- If two sides of a triangle have lengths 5 and 9, the third side $x$ must satisfy: $9 - 5 < x < 9 + 5$, i.e., $4 < x < 14$.
- The third side cannot equal 4 or 14 — it must be strictly between those values.
- This theorem is useful for determining whether three given lengths can form a triangle, and for finding the range of possible values for an unknown side.

---

### 2.2.8 — Similar Triangles

**Definition:** Two triangles are similar if their corresponding angles are congruent and their corresponding sides are proportional.

**Similarity Postulates:**

- **AA (Angle-Angle):** If two angles of one triangle are congruent to two angles of another triangle, the triangles are similar. (The third angle must also be congruent because the sum of angles in a triangle is always $180^\circ$.)
- **SSS (Side-Side-Side):** If all three pairs of corresponding sides are proportional, the triangles are similar.
- **SAS (Side-Angle-Side):** If two pairs of corresponding sides are proportional and the included angles are congruent, the triangles are similar.

**Proportion Setup:** If $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle DEF$, then:

$$\frac{AB}{DE} = \frac{BC}{EF} = \frac{AC}{DF}$$

**Critical details:**

- The order of vertices matters. If $\triangle ABC \sim \triangle DEF$, then $A$ corresponds to $D$, $B$ corresponds to $E$, and $C$ corresponds to $F$.
- Similar triangles have the same shape but not necessarily the same size.
- The ratio of corresponding sides is called the **scale factor**.
- The ratio of their areas is the **square** of the scale factor.
- The ratio of their perimeters equals the scale factor.

---

### 2.2.9 — Volume Formulas

**Rectangular Prism (Box):** $V = lwh$ (length × width × height)

**Cube:** $V = s^3$ (where $s$ = side length)

**Right Circular Cylinder:** $V = \pi r^2 h$ (area of circular base × height)

**Sphere:** $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$

**Right Circular Cone:** $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ (one-third the volume of a cylinder with the same base and height)

**Rectangular Pyramid:** $V = \frac{1}{3}lwh$

**Prism (general):** $V = Bh$ (area of base × height, where the base can be any shape)

**Critical details:**

- For cylinders, cones, and spheres, the radius $r$ is the key measurement. If you are given the diameter, remember to divide by 2 to get the radius.
- The height of a cylinder or cone must be the perpendicular height, not the slant height.
- The volume of a cone is exactly one-third the volume of a cylinder with the same base radius and height. This is a fact the ACT sometimes tests directly.

---

### 2.2.10 — Surface Area Formulas

**Rectangular Prism:** $SA = 2lw + 2lh + 2wh$ (sum of the areas of all 6 faces)

**Cube:** $SA = 6s^2$

**Sphere:** $SA = 4\pi r^2$

**Right Circular Cylinder:** $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ (area of the two circular bases + area of the lateral surface)

**Right Circular Cone:** $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi rl$ (area of the base + lateral surface area, where $l$ is the slant height)

**Critical details:**

- Surface area is the total area of all exterior faces of a three-dimensional object.
- For a cylinder, the lateral surface "unrolls" into a rectangle with width equal to the circumference of the base ($2\pi r$) and height $h$.
- For a cone, the slant height $l$ is found using the Pythagorean Theorem: $l = \sqrt{r^2 + h^2}$.

---

### 2.2.11 — Inscribed and Circumscribed Figures

These configurations appear frequently on the ACT and require you to combine circle formulas with triangle or quadrilateral formulas.

**Triangle Inscribed in a Semicircle:** Any triangle inscribed in a circle such that one side is the diameter is a **right triangle** (Thales' Theorem). The right angle is at the vertex opposite the diameter.

**Square Inscribed in a Circle:** The diagonal of the square equals the diameter of the circle ($2r$). The side length of the square is $s = r\sqrt{2}$. The area of the square is $2r^2$.

**Circle Inscribed in a Square:** The diameter of the circle equals the side length of the square. The radius is $r = \frac{s}{2}$. The area of the circle is $\pi r^2$ and the area of the square is $4r^2$.

**Equilateral Triangle Inscribed in a Circle of Radius $r$:**
- Side length: $s = r\sqrt{3}$
- Area: $\frac{3\sqrt{3}}{4}r^2$
- Height: $\frac{3}{2}r$

**30°-60°-90° Triangle Inscribed in a Circle:** The hypotenuse is the diameter ($2r$). The shorter leg (opposite 30°) equals $r$, and the longer leg (opposite 60°) equals $r\sqrt{3}$.

---

## 2.3 — Algebra and Advanced Math Formulas

---

### 2.3.1 — Quadratic Formula

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

**What it means:** For any quadratic equation in standard form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ (where $a \neq 0$), this formula gives the solutions (roots) of the equation.

**Critical details:**

- The expression under the square root, $b^2 - 4ac$, is called the **discriminant**. It determines the number and type of solutions:
  - $b^2 - 4ac > 0$: Two distinct real solutions
  - $b^2 - 4ac = 0$: Exactly one real solution (a repeated root)
  - $b^2 - 4ac < 0$: No real solutions (two complex solutions)
- The $\pm$ symbol means you compute two values: one with $+$ and one with $-$.
- The quadratic formula works for **any** quadratic equation, even those that cannot be factored easily.
- Before applying the formula, make sure the equation is in standard form $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with all terms on one side.

**ACT trap:** A very common error is forgetting that the denominator is $2a$, not just $2$. Another common error is sign mistakes when substituting negative values for $b$ or $c$. Use parentheses when entering values into your calculator.

---

### 2.3.2 — Vertex of a Parabola

For a quadratic in standard form $y = ax^2 + bx + c$:

**$x$-coordinate of the vertex:** $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$

**$y$-coordinate of the vertex:** Substitute $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$ back into the equation.

**Critical details:**

- The vertex is the **maximum** point of the parabola if $a < 0$ (opens downward).
- The vertex is the **minimum** point of the parabola if $a > 0$ (opens upward).
- The axis of symmetry is the vertical line $x = -\frac{b}{2a}$.
- The vertex form of a quadratic, $y = a(x - h)^2 + k$, makes the vertex immediately visible as $(h, k)$.

---

### 2.3.3 — Sum and Product of Roots

For the quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $r_1$ and $r_2$:

**Sum of roots:** $r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{b}{a}$

**Product of roots:** $r_1 \cdot r_2 = \frac{c}{a}$

**Critical details:**

- These relationships (known as Vieta's formulas) allow you to find the sum and product of the roots without actually solving for the roots individually.
- The ACT frequently tests these relationships. For example, a question might ask: "What is the sum of the solutions of $2x^2 - 7x + 3 = 0$?" The answer is $-\frac{-7}{2} = \frac{7}{2}$.
- If you know the sum and product of the roots, you can reconstruct the quadratic: $x^2 - (\text{sum})x + (\text{product}) = 0$.

---

### 2.3.4 — Factoring Patterns

**Greatest Common Factor (GCF):** $ab + ac = a(b + c)$

**Difference of Squares:** $a^2 - b^2 = (a - b)(a + b)$

**Difference of Cubes:** $a^3 - b^3 = (a - b)(a^2 + ab + b^2)$

**Sum of Cubes:** $a^3 + b^3 = (a + b)(a^2 - ab + b^2)$

**Perfect Square Trinomials:**
- $a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = (a + b)^2$
- $a^2 - 2ab + b^2 = (a - b)^2$

**General Trinomial Factoring:** $x^2 + bx + c = (x + m)(x + n)$ where $m + n = b$ and $mn = c$.

**Factoring by Grouping (for 4-term polynomials):** $ax + ay + bx + by = a(x + y) + b(x + y) = (a + b)(x + y)$

**Critical details:**

- The difference of squares is the most commonly tested factoring pattern on the ACT. Recognize it instantly: any expression of the form (something)$^2$ minus (something else)$^2$ factors as the product of the sum and difference.
- The sum and difference of cubes are less common but do appear, especially on harder questions.
- A common mistake is trying to factor a sum of squares ($a^2 + b^2$) over the real numbers — it does not factor. This is an irreducible polynomial in the real number system.

---

### 2.3.5 — Exponent Rules

**Product Rule:** $x^a \cdot x^b = x^{a+b}$

**Quotient Rule:** $\frac{x^a}{x^b} = x^{a-b}$

**Power Rule:** $(x^a)^b = x^{ab}$

**Zero Exponent:** $x^0 = 1$ (for any $x \neq 0$)

**Negative Exponent:** $x^{-a} = \frac{1}{x^a}$

**Fractional Exponent:** $x^{a/b} = \sqrt[b]{x^a} = (\sqrt[b]{x})^a$

**Critical details:**

- These rules apply when the base is the same. You cannot combine $2^3 \cdot 3^2$ using the product rule because the bases differ.
- A negative exponent does not make the result negative — it indicates a reciprocal. For example, $2^{-3} = \frac{1}{8}$, not $-8$.
- Fractional exponents connect directly to radicals: $x^{1/2} = \sqrt{x}$, $x^{1/3} = \sqrt[3]{x}$, etc.
- When simplifying expressions with exponents, apply the rules systematically. Do not skip steps.

**ACT trap:** The test frequently includes wrong answer choices that result from common exponent errors, such as $(x^a)^b = x^{a+b}$ (incorrect — should be $x^{ab}$) or $x^a \cdot x^b = x^{ab}$ (incorrect — should be $x^{a+b}$).

---

### 2.3.6 — Radical Rules

**Product Rule:** $\sqrt{a} \cdot \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{ab}$

**Quotient Rule:** $\frac{\sqrt{a}}{\sqrt{b}} = \sqrt{\frac{a}{b}}$

**Simplifying Radicals:** Factor out perfect squares (or perfect cubes, etc.) from under the radical.

**Rationalizing the Denominator:** If a fraction has a radical in the denominator, multiply numerator and denominator by that radical to eliminate it.

**Example:** $\frac{3}{\sqrt{5}} = \frac{3\sqrt{5}}{5}$

**Critical details:**

- You can only add or subtract radicals if they have the same radicand (the expression under the radical). For example, $3\sqrt{2} + 5\sqrt{2} = 8\sqrt{2}$, but $3\sqrt{2} + 5\sqrt{3}$ cannot be simplified further.
- When simplifying $\sqrt{72}$, factor out the largest perfect square: $\sqrt{72} = \sqrt{36 \cdot 2} = 6\sqrt{2}$.

---

### 2.3.7 — Logarithm Rules

**Definition:** $\log_b(x) = y$ means $b^y = x$. The logarithm is the exponent to which the base must be raised to produce the argument.

**Product Rule:** $\log_b(xy) = \log_b(x) + \log_b(y)$

**Quotient Rule:** $\log_b\left(\frac{x}{y}\right) = \log_b(x) - \log_b(y)$

**Power Rule:** $\log_b(x^y) = y \cdot \log_b(x)$

**Change of Base Formula:** $\log_b(x) = \frac{\log_c(x)}{\log_c(b)}$ (commonly used with base 10 or base $e$)

**Common Logarithm:** $\log(x)$ (base 10) — the "log" button on most calculators

**Natural Logarithm:** $\ln(x)$ (base $e \approx 2.71828$) — the "ln" button on most calculators

**Special Values:**
- $\log_b(1) = 0$ for any base $b$ (because $b^0 = 1$)
- $\log_b(b) = 1$ for any base $b$ (because $b^1 = b$)
- $\log_b(b^x) = x$

**Critical details:**

- Logarithms appear on the ACT primarily in the context of solving exponential equations and understanding logarithmic functions.
- The domain of $\log_b(x)$ is $x > 0$ — you cannot take the logarithm of zero or a negative number.
- If $\log_b(x) = \log_b(y)$, then $x = y$ (the logarithmic function is one-to-one).

---

### 2.3.8 — Absolute Value

**Definition:** $|x| = \begin{cases} x & \text{if } x \ge 0 \\ -x & \text{if } x < 0 \end{cases}$

**Geometric interpretation:** $|x|$ is the distance from $x$ to 0 on the number line. More generally, $|x - a|$ is the distance from $x$ to $a$ on the number line.

**Absolute Value Equations:** If $|x| = a$ (where $a > 0$), then $x = a$ or $x = -a$.

**Absolute Value Inequalities:**
- $|x| < a$ means $-a < x < a$ (AND condition)
- $|x| > a$ means $x < -a$ or $x > a$ (OR condition)

**Critical details:**

- The geometric interpretation of absolute value as distance is extremely useful on the ACT. If a question asks for $|x - 3| < 5$, you can interpret this as "the distance from $x$ to 3 is less than 5," which immediately gives $-2 < x < 8$.
- When solving absolute value equations, always check for extraneous solutions.

---

### 2.3.9 — Complex Numbers

**Definition:** $i = \sqrt{-1}$, and $i^2 = -1$.

**Powers of $i$ (cyclic pattern):**
- $i^1 = i$
- $i^2 = -1$
- $i^3 = -i$
- $i^4 = 1$
- $i^5 = i$ (cycle repeats)

**General rule:** To find $i^n$, divide $n$ by 4 and use the remainder:
- Remainder 0 → $i^n = 1$
- Remainder 1 → $i^n = i$
- Remainder 2 → $i^n = -1$
- Remainder 3 → $i^n = -i$

**Complex number arithmetic:** A complex number has the form $a + bi$, where $a$ is the real part and $b$ is the imaginary part.

- Addition: $(a + bi) + (c + di) = (a + c) + (b + d)i$
- Multiplication (FOIL): $(a + bi)(c + di) = ac + adi + bci + bdi^2 = (ac - bd) + (ad + bc)i$ (using $i^2 = -1$)

**Critical details:**

- Complex numbers appear on the ACT but are not heavily tested. Typically, 1–2 questions involve basic complex number operations.
- The key insight is that $i^2 = -1$. Every time you encounter $i^2$ in a computation, replace it with $-1$.

---

## 2.4 — Trigonometry Formulas

Trigonometry accounts for approximately 4–7% of ACT Math questions (roughly 3–4 questions on the legacy 60-question test). While this seems small, these questions are highly formula-dependent, meaning memorization directly translates to points.

---

### 2.4.1 — Right Triangle Trigonometry (SOH CAH TOA)

$$\sin(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\cos(\theta) = \frac{\text{Adjacent}}{\text{Hypotenuse}}$$

$$\tan(\theta) = \frac{\text{Opposite}}{\text{Adjacent}}$$

**Critical details:**

- "Opposite" means the side opposite the angle $\theta$.
- "Adjacent" means the side next to the angle $\theta$ that is **not** the hypotenuse.
- "Hypotenuse" is always the longest side, opposite the right angle.
- These ratios apply **only** to right triangles.
- To find an angle given a trig ratio, use the inverse functions: $\sin^{-1}$, $\cos^{-1}$, $\tan^{-1}$ (also written as arcsin, arccos, arctan).

**Mnemonic:** Some People Have, Curly Black Hair, Through Proper Brushing. (Some = Sin, People = Opposite, Have = Hypotenuse, etc.) Or simply: SOH CAH TOA.

---

### 2.4.2 — Reciprocal Trigonometric Functions

$$\csc(\theta) = \frac{1}{\sin(\theta)} = \frac{\text{Hypotenuse}}{\text{Opposite}}$$
